  Per the plan ("one stdlib module at a time, build stays green"), let me survey
  which stdlib modules still have non-foreign procs to convert next:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Most ;-terminated ones are #foreign externs (they stay). The real conversion
  targets are the proc definitions ({ bodies): io (fread_line, fd_autoclose,
  skip_header, file_map), csv.load, http (parse_request_line, respond,

  Per the plan, io ‚Üí io-as-data is the representative next module. Let me read
  it:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ io has 4 non-foreign procs. Two are genuinely blocked: file_map needs the
  deferred zip|>fin slice-return mechanism (same gap that keeps os.argv a proc,
  per funceff-deferred-codegen-gaps), and fd_autoclose is the @drop hook the
  plan says to keep. fread_line/skip_header need the io-as-data redesign. Let me
  gauge the blast radius before committing to a design ‚ÄĒ who calls these across
  the corpus:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Blast radius is tiny ‚ÄĒ fread_line has zero external callers (only skip_header
  uses it internally), skip_header has 1 caller, file_map has 2. The real
  leverage: file_map, csv.load, and os.argv all share one deferred codegen gap ‚ÄĒ
  zip|>fin with slice slots/returns. Building that mechanism unblocks all three
  at once ("mechanisms first"). Let me read how os.argv is written today to
  assess that mechanism:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ os.argv as a func would be zip(os_argv(i), os_argv_len(i)) |> slice_to where
  slice_to(raw: []char, n: i64) -> []char ‚ÄĒ a finalizer with a slice input AND 
  slice return. zip|>fin works for scalar finalizers (gfx.frame) and slice-IN
  works (ms_of); the gap is the slice return. This one keystone unblocks os.argv
  + io.file_map + csv.load. Let me assess feasibility by reading how |>
  finalizers + Eff runs are lowered:

  Read 1 file, ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)
‚óŹ The deferred case (7642-7667) is a genuine design fork, not just missing
  codegen: os.argv's raw[0:n] needs to form a checked slice from an unbounded
  foreign pointer + a dynamic length ‚ÄĒ which fights arche's checked-slice safety
  invariant ("no sound length to hand the cook's raw param"). The user reserved
  that kind of call ("I will tell you what to actually do").

  But crucially ‚ÄĒ the approved plan doesn't require that mechanism. For io it
  says "Replaces fread_line/skip_header/file_map" with an io-as-data read system
  filling a buffer column (fixed width N, no dynamic slice-from-raw-pointer).
  That sidesteps the fork entirely. Let me read csv (entangled via file_map) to
  scope the io+csv conversion:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ csv.load is a generic proc ‚ÄĒ proc(arch: archetype, ...) using #each_field
  reflection to populate any archetype's columns. Turning that into a scheduled
  "system" (which can't take an archetype arg) is a real design fork, as is the
  slice-safety fork for file_map/os.argv. These are exactly the decisions the
  user reserved.

  Let me look at the one skip_header caller to find the smallest unambiguous
  io-as-data step the plan does spell out:

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

  read loop ‚ÄĒ it's newlines - 1 in the same scan. Let me check the fixture size
  to confirm a single chunk covers it:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

  io-as-data, no per-char proc. Note io.read_chunk (func‚ÜíEff) already fills the
  column; only the sequential fread_line/skip_header procs are non-conforming,
  and they now have ~0 callers. Let me convert the test:

‚óŹ Everything is green and clean (865/865, corpus, fmt). Here's where the

  Done this session:
  - Router module fully converted ‚ÄĒ flat-table logic, app owns the Route/Req

  deliberately errors there (zip(‚Ä¶) |> fin with a slice return) and these stay
  procs. You said you'd tell me what to actually do rather than have me invent a

‚óŹ Last piece confirmed: intrinsics are declared @intrinsic in a #foreign block
  (sets an is_intrinsic flag), recognized by that flag in codegen
  (codegen.c:4968), and dispatched by name/arity to a custom IR emitter. Adding
  a new builtin = declare it + add one codegen branch. @syscall(N) works the
  same way via syscall_num.

  I now have the full picture. Here's the synthesis.

  What already exists for "FFI pointer ‚Üí bounded array"

  The conversion primitive is already there: the slice operator ptr[0:len].
  - codegen_slice() (codegen.c:2131-2254) takes a base pointer + a runtime i64
  length and emits a fully bounds-checked (ptr, len) slice. This is
  pointer-plus-length ‚Üí bounded array.
  - A foreign raw pointer is modeled as a type-6 ValueInfo with string_len = -1
  (the "no static length" marker). Slicing it (raw[0:n]) deliberately bypasses 
  the bounds-check and trusts n (codegen.c:2213) ‚ÄĒ exactly the FFI contract "the
  runtime told me the length."
  - Slices are (ptr, len) fat pointers everywhere: funcs receive them as two
  args (%arg.ptr, %arg.len), return them as {T*, i64} aggregates, and .length
  reads the len half. All the insertvalue/extractvalue plumbing exists.

  So os.argv/io.file_map already do the real conversion (raw[0:n]) inside a
  proc. Nothing about the conversion is unsound or missing ‚ÄĒ the proc form
  compiles fine today.

  The only actual gap is that the pure-func/finalizer form (zip(os_argv, 
  os_argv_len) |> slice_to) is blanket-rejected (codegen.c:7661) because the
  extern's []char out-slot is a {i8*, i64} aggregate and nobody wrote the code
  to unpack it into the finalizer's two-arg (ptr,len) ABI. It's an ABI-bridging
  stub, not a safety wall.

  One real soundness smell surfaced: os_argv is declared (s: []char) but C only
  returns a char* ‚ÄĒ so the len half of that "slice" is garbage. The proc gets
  away with it because raw[0:n] ignores raw's bogus length and uses n. A clean
  reusable design should stop pretending a bare FFI pointer is a slice.

  Three coherent designs (all reuse codegen_slice)

  on it, and routing around the checked-slice model entirely. That's a real new
  precedent and the wrong direction.

  already lives ‚ÄĒ the C/runtime glue for the mmap path:

  Ran 2 shell commands (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ That settles it. Here's what the constraints actually force, and my
  recommendation.

  The key realization: there's already a sound precedent, and it needs no new 
  type

  The codebase already has a sound "FFI buffer + length ‚Üí checked slice" pattern
  ‚ÄĒ sys_read: it surfaces a buffer + a byte-count as out-slots, and the caller
  writes buf[0:count]. That works in pure funcs today (io.read is a func ‚Üí 
  Eff([]char, i64)), with zero new types. The unsafe length lives at the
  #foreign boundary; arche-side it's a normal (ptr, len) slice.

  file_map/os.argv only differ because their buffer is callee-allocated (mmap /
  argv own the memory, so there's no arche-side capacity to bounds-check
  against) and E0223 limits a C extern to one return value ‚ÄĒ so the length comes
  from a second #foreign call (arche_file_size/os_argv_len). That forces the
  applicative zip(ptr, len) |> bound shape, which hits the one unimplemented
  stub (the finalizer aggregate-unpack at codegen.c:7661). That's the whole gap.
  The conversion itself (p[0:n], via codegen_slice) already exists and is
  already used.

  How we prevent abuse ‚ÄĒ confine by value-flow, not a nameable type

  Your instinct is right: no rawptr type. Instead:

  1. The only producer of an unbounded foreign pointer is a #foreign extern
  out-slot. User code cannot construct one ‚ÄĒ #foreign is already the trust gate.
  2. No nameable type ‚ÄĒ so it can't be declared, stored in a pool column, or
  arithmetic'd. It exists only as a transient value inside the zip(...) |> bound
  pipeline, immediately consumed by the bounding slice.
  3. Hard-error guard (matching your "hard errors over lints" rule): a value
  carrying the "untrusted length" marker (type-6, string_len == -1) has its only
  legal operation be a slice with an explicit hi bound (p[0:n]). Reading
  .length, a bare p[i], returning it un-sliced, or binding it to a normal local
  = a hard E-error. This is strictly safer than today, where raw.length silently
  returns garbage.
  4. The bound(p, n) -> []char { return p[0:n]; } primitive lives in one stdlib
  FFI module (e.g. mem/ffi), reused by os/io/csv. It's the single audited
  consumer.

  So the unsafe ptr‚Üílen pairing stays exactly where it already is (the C glue:
  mmap+fstat, argv+strlen), and arche never gains a general escape hatch ‚ÄĒ just
  the ability to bound a foreign pointer in a pure func instead of only in a
  proc.

  Concrete plan: (a) implement the finalizer aggregate-unpack at codegen.c:7661
  so zip(p, n) |> bound compiles; (b) add the hard-error guard restricting
  untrusted-length (string_len == -1) slices to explicit-bound slicing only; (c)
  add the bound primitive + rewrite os.argv, io.file_map, and csv.load's mmap
  to pure funcs; (d) keep the unsafe length in the C glue as-is.

  Before I build it ‚ÄĒ two things genuinely worth your call:

‚óŹ Got it ‚ÄĒ that's a much better answer than my rawptr-as-pointer idea. rawptr as
  an alias for i64 makes a foreign address an inert integer: you can't deref
  it, can't index it, can't .length it ‚ÄĒ the type system already forbids every
  memory operation. So "bound is the only flow" becomes structural, not a lint
  we have to enforce: the single primitive that interprets an i64 as memory
  (inttoptr + form a checked (ptr,len) slice) is the only door, and there's no
  other way through. And nicely, with rawptr = i64 the finalizer's args are now
  both scalar ‚ÄĒ only the slice return needs new codegen, which shrinks the
  problem.

‚óŹ Memory updated. Here's where things stand, then your formatter question.

  Landed this session (all green: 866 tests + corpus -Werror + fmt)

  The FFI pointer‚Üíslice mechanism you specified is built and working. rawptr is
  an inert i64 alias (stdlib/mem), and mem.bound(p, n) ‚ÄĒ an @intrinsic pure func
  ‚ÄĒ is the single audited door that turns a foreign address + length into a
  checked []char (inttoptr + the existing codegen_slice machinery). What I had
  to add:
  - is_intrinsic on funcs (so bound is a pure primitive usable as a |>
  finalizer);
  - bound's inline codegen + the zip(‚Ä¶) |> fin slice-return path (the deferred
  gap, now resolved for the intrinsic finalizer);
  - qualified finalizer resolution (mem.bound in |> no longer flagged
  undefined);
  - the safety guarantee made structural: slicing a scalar (incl. rawptr) is now
  a hard error (E0201 extended to slice exprs). raw[0:n] on a foreign address
  won't compile ‚ÄĒ bound is the only flow, enforced by the type system, not a
  lint.

  Converted to pure funcs (no longer procs): os.argv and io.file_map, both now
  zip(addr, len) |> mem.bound. Verified end-to-end (real argv bytes, real mmap'd
  file). Regression tests added: argv_bound_slice, plus the rawptr-slice
  hard-error is exercised by the corrected char_arg_passthrough.

  Still a proc: csv.load ‚ÄĒ it's the archetype-generic loader (proc(arch: 
  archetype, ‚Ä¶) + #each_field reflection), which is a genuinely different fork
  (how a reflection-driven loader becomes a scheduled system), not a
  slice-safety one. I'd want to treat that on its own.

  Your formatter question ‚ÄĒ |> top-to-bottom

  Doable, and worth doing. My take on the policy (it touches every |> in the
  tree, so it's worth getting right):
  - Break a chain onto continuation lines only when it has ‚Č•2 |> or the 
  one-liner exceeds the line width; keep short single-pipe forms like
  clock_mono() |> ms_of inline. A leading-operator layout reads best:
  zip(os_argv(i), os_argv_len(i))
    |> mem.bound
  You said "later" ‚ÄĒ so I've left it out of this change to keep the
  proc-elimination diff clean. Want me to pick it up next as a focused formatter

  The two remaining stdlib procs are genuine forks (both have no gated callers):
  1. csv.load ‚ÄĒ a compile-time-reflective archetype-generic loader (proc(arch: 
  archetype) + #each_field). It fits neither a func (pure-only) nor a system (no
  args). My recommendation: treat it as a csv-device primitive and exempt it 
  from the proc lint (it is compile-time codegen over reflection ‚ÄĒ a primitive,
  like @intrinsic). The alternative is killing the generic API and making every
  app write its own load system ‚ÄĒ bigger, and it churns the benchmark callers.
  2. http.respond ‚ÄĒ socket writes + a variable-length body. The io-as-data form
  needs a response/body pool model and a network test harness to verify (neither
  exists). It's effectively dead code. My recommendation: leave it for whenever
  a socket-io-as-data design is actually needed.

  If you take both recommendations, the stdlib proc-elimination is effectively
  complete (everything left is a primitive, exempt, or dead), which unblocks
  Phase 0 (promote the proc lints to errors). The other open phase is Phase 3
  (remove main ‚Üí force #run) ‚ÄĒ that one's a large mechanical sweep across ~640
  test files and is independent of the above.

  Want me to (a) wire up the csv.load exemption + promote the lints, or (b)
  start the main‚Üí#run sweep?

‚óŹ Now I deeply understand the target model. arche-rpg is pure io-as-data:
  - No procs, no main ‚ÄĒ the whole program is #run { seq({ boot, forever(seq({ 
  systems‚Ä¶ })) }) } over pool declarations.
  - Systems/maps/eaches operate on pools; data flows through columns (system 1
  writes last, system 2 reads it).

  - Branching via pool membership: done :: each (Closed) runs zero times until
  gfx.poll inserts a Closed row ‚ÄĒ that's how conditionals become data.

          - **and** mutable global bindings

          - visible across the whole file

       55 +  if (!ok) { respond(conn, 400); return; }       // guard     
       56 +  resolve(target)(id:);                                       
       57 +  if (id < 0) { respond(conn, 404); return; }     // guard    
       58 +  serve(conn, target);                            // ‚Ä¶and so o
          +n, every step gated by the previous                           
       59 +}                                                             
       60 +```                                                           
       61  
       62 +**Do** let a producer route rows into a pool, and a consumer p
          +rocess whatever is there ‚ÄĒ with no                            
       63 +"is there anything?" check:                                   
       64 +                                                              
       65  ```arche
       61 -fmt.assert(entry() == 41, "forward references should resolve\n
          -");                                                           
       66 +#import { fmt }                                               
       67 +                                                              
       68 +Input :: arche { in_v :: int }                                
       69 +Ready :: arche { out_v :: int }                               
       70 +[4]Input;                                                     
       71 +[4]Ready;                                                     
       72 +                                                              
       73 +seed :: system {                                              
       74 +  insert(Input { in_v: 5 })(_:, _:);                          
       75 +  insert(Input { in_v: -3 })(_:, _:);                         
       76 +  insert(Input { in_v: 7 })(_:, _:);                          
       77 +}                                                             
       78 +                                                              
       79 +// PRODUCER: route only the positive inputs into Ready; a nega
          +tive inserts no row.                                          
       80 +produce :: each (query { in_v }) {                            
       81 +  if (in_v > 0) {                                             
       82 +    insert(Ready { out_v: in_v })(_:, _:);                    
       83 +  }                                                           
       84 +}                                                             
       85 +                                                              
       86 +// CONSUMER: runs once per Ready row ‚ÄĒ and ZERO times if none 
          +were produced. No "if anything ready" guard.                  
       87 +consume :: each (query { out_v }) {                           
       88 +  fmt.assert(out_v > 0, "every Ready row came from a positive 
          +input\n");                                                    
       89 +}                                                             
       90 +                                                              
       91 +#run seq({ seed, produce, consume })                          
       92  ```
       93  
       64 -**Why it matters:** this is what lets the visibility-band idio
          -m (public API on top, internals behind                        
       65 -a trailing banner) coexist with the helpers a public proc uses
          -. Mutable global bindings                                     
       66 -(`name : T[N]` buffers, `name := v` scalars) hoist the same wa
          -y. There is no "declare it first"                             
       67 -constraint at top level ‚ÄĒ only locals, inside a proc/func body
          -, are point-of-introduction (they have                        
       68 -control flow and lifetime).                                   
       94 +**Why:** a consumer `each` over an empty pool is a no-op ‚ÄĒ tha
          +t *is* the conditional. System 1                              
       95 +produces `n` (writes a row/column); system 2 consumes `n` and 
          +does the n+1 step; the schedule orders                        
       96 +writer-before-reader within the pass. "Branch on a result" bec
          +omes "route the row into the pool for                         
       97 +that case," and an absent row is simply never read ‚ÄĒ no per-st
          +ep guards, no intra-row effect‚Üíeffect                         
       98 +chaining. This is the shape of arche-rpg's `done :: each (Clos
          +ed)`: it fires only once a `Closed` row                       
       99 +exists, so "should we exit?" needs no boolean ‚ÄĒ the row's pres
          +ence is the signal.                                           
      100

       96  
       97  ## Returning a variable number of results without a heap
       98  
       99 -There is no heap: a proc cannot allocate an array and return i
          -t, and returning a pointer into its                           
      100 -own frame would dangle. So the storage for a variable-length r
          -esult must be **pre-reserved by the                           
      101 -caller or by the module** ‚ÄĒ a fixed, bounded slot either way. 
       99 +There is no heap: a `func` cannot allocate an array and return
          + a pointer into its own frame ‚ÄĒ that                          
      100 +would dangle. So a variable-length result must live in storage
          + **pre-reserved by the caller** (a                            
      101 +fixed, bounded slot), or in a **pool** ‚ÄĒ never a freshly-alloc
          +ated return value.                                            
      102  
      103 -**Don't** try to hand back a freshly-made array:              
      103 +**Don't** try to hand back a slice of a fresh local ‚ÄĒ its stor
          +age is the frame, so it is rejected:                          
      104  
      105  ```
      106 -resolve :: proc(path: []char)(starts: []int, lens: []int, coun
          -t: int)   // no storage exists for starts/lens                
      106 +mk_squares :: func(n: int) -> []int {                         
      107 +  xs : [16]int;            // a fresh local ‚ÄĒ lives in this fu
          +nc's frame                                                    
      108 +  for (i := 0; i < n; i += 1) { xs[i] = i * i; }              
      109 +  return xs[0: n];         // REJECTED: the slice would dangle
          + once the frame is gone                                       
      110 +}                                                             
      111  ```
      112  
      113  **Do** thread an **owned slice** (`own T[]`): the caller `move
           `s a bounded buffer in, the func

      131  fmt.assert(out[3] == 9, "fill_squares broken\n");   // .length
            flows back with the slice
      132  ```
      133  
      134 -Alternatives to the owned slice: (a) write into a caller-owned
          -, bounded buffer passed by reference                          
      135 -(both in ‚ÄĒ "here's my storage" ‚ÄĒ and out ‚ÄĒ "now it's filled", 
          -the shadow of `io.fread`); or (b)                             
      136 -record into module-level state and expose accessors (what `rou
          -ter` does ‚ÄĒ `cap_start[8]` /                                  
      137 -`cap_len[8]` / `cap_count[1]`, read back via `param(i)` / `par
          -am_count()`).                                                 
      134 +Two more options, both heap-free: (a) **a span** ‚ÄĒ return offs
          +et+length `int`s into the caller's own                        
      135 +input, copying nothing (what `http.parse_request_line` writes 
          +onto each request row: `method_start` /                       
      136 +`method_len`); (b) **a pool** ‚ÄĒ when the result is "many rows,
          +" don't return it at all: a producer                          
      137 +system `insert`s the rows and a consumer reads them (see "Prod
          +ucer / consumer" above). The `[N]T`                           
      138 +pool is the bounded, static storage; the rows ARE the result. 
      139  
      139 -A func may return a slice only when it traces to a buffer pass
          -ed _in_ ‚ÄĒ returning a slice of a                              
      140 -fresh local would dangle and is rejected. (See language.md, "A
          -rrays and slices".)                                           
      140 +A func may return a slice only when it traces to a buffer pass
          +ed _in_ ‚ÄĒ a slice of a fresh local is                         
      141 +rejected (see language.md, "Arrays and slices").              
      142  
      143  **Why:** "bounded" is the load-bearing word ‚ÄĒ a compile-time-k
           nown size is what lets the slot be a
      143 -fixed stack or static reservation instead of a heap allocation
          -. The count is a plain `int` and                              
      144 -returns normally; only the _array_ part needs the reservation.
          - Caller-buffer (reentrant, per-call)                          
      145 -vs module-static (one shared slot, overwritten by the next cal
          -l) is just _where_ that fixed slot                            
      146 -lives. Spans (offset+length into the caller's own input) keep 
          -it zero-copy: nothing is owned or                             
      147 -duplicated.                                                   
      144 +fixed stack or static reservation instead of a heap allocation
          +. The owned slice keeps the storage in                        
      145 +the caller's buffer; a span returns plain `int`s into the inpu
          +t; a pool holds the rows. None of these                       
      146 +is a `proc` ‚ÄĒ in arche, results that grow live in pools (rows)
          +, and effects live in systems over those                      
      147 +pools, not in a callee's out-params.

‚óŹ Hit the real frontier (exactly what you predicted ‚ÄĒ "might need some new
  features"). Three constraints surfaced:
  1. A queried buffer column is read-only ‚ÄĒ raw[r]=0, path[n]=..., move body are

  2. insert requires every column (no partial insert).

‚ĚĮ the idiom is to not access B directly ever.                                   

‚óŹ Understood ‚ÄĒ never touch B directly; everything goes through a query. That
  rules out my B.buf[0][...] workaround, and it sharpens the buffer-write idiom:
  a buffer column is filled only by an Eff out-slot (recv, sprintf, read) and
  read by slicing (raw[a:b]) ‚ÄĒ never manual element writes, never direct pool
  access. Lengths live in scalar columns. Let me check fmt.sprintf's span-copy
  ability, since that's the one buffer-write I have left (materializing the
  target span):

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ That sharpens the whole idiom: a buffer column is produced only by an Eff 
  out-slot (recv, io.read, fmt.sprintf) and consumed by slicing ‚ÄĒ never manual
  element writes, never direct Conn.x access. So my recv/materialize/put were
  all wrong (manual buffer writes), and the fix is to push every buffer through
  an Eff and keep lengths in scalar columns.

  That collapses most of the web server cleanly:
  - recv ‚Üí net.recv(...)(raw, r:) fills raw, r goes to a scalar req_len (no
  NUL-terminate; parse reads req_len, not strlen).
  - file body ‚Üí io.read(fd, ...)(content:) fills body (already an out-slot).
  - response ‚Üí fmt.sprintf(...)(hdr, n:) (already an out-slot).

  The one thing that doesn't reduce to an out-slot is materializing the target 
  span into a path buffer for the router ‚ÄĒ copying raw[target_start : 
  target_start+target_len] into another column. Within "no manual buffer
  writes," there are two idiomatic ways and they shape the stdlib APIs
  differently ‚ÄĒ your call:

      103  that case," and an absent row is simply never read ‚ÄĒ no per-st
           ep guards, no intra-row effect‚Üíeffect

           ence is the signal.
      106 -                                                              
      107 -## Returning a variable number of results without a heap      
      108 -                                                              
      109 -There is no heap: a `func` cannot allocate an array and return
          - a pointer into its own frame ‚ÄĒ that                          
      110 -would dangle. So a variable-length result must live in storage
          - **pre-reserved by the caller** (a                            
      111 -fixed, bounded slot), or in a **pool** ‚ÄĒ never a freshly-alloc
          -ated return value.                                            
      112 -                                                              
      113 -**Don't** try to hand back a slice of a fresh local ‚ÄĒ its stor
          -age is the frame, so it is rejected:                          
      114 -                                                              
      115 -```                                                           
      116 -mk_squares :: func(n: int) -> []int {                         
      117 -  xs : [16]int;            // a fresh local ‚ÄĒ lives in this fu
          -nc's frame                                                    
      118 -  for (i := 0; i < n; i += 1) { xs[i] = i * i; }              
      119 -  return xs[0: n];         // REJECTED: the slice would dangle
          - once the frame is gone                                       
      120 -}                                                             
      121 -```                                                           
      122 -                                                              
      123 -**Do** thread an **owned slice** (`own T[]`): the caller `move
          -`s a bounded buffer in, the func                              
      124 -fills it and hands the fat pointer back, the caller rebinds it
          -. The runtime length rides in the                             
      125 -slice, so no size appears in the signature, and there is still
          - no heap ‚ÄĒ the storage is the caller's                        
      126 -buffer throughout:                                            
      127 -                                                              
      128 -```arche                                                      
      129 -fill_squares :: func(own xs: []int, n: int) -> []int {        
      130 -  i := 0;                                                     
      131 -  for (; i < n; i += 1) {                                     
      132 -    xs[i] = i * i;                                            
      133 -  }                                                           
      134 -  return xs;                                                  
      135 -}                                                             
      136 -```                                                           
      137 -                                                              
      138 -```arche                                                      
      139 -buf : [16]int;                                                
      140 -out := fill_squares(move buf, 4);                             
      141 -fmt.assert(out[3] == 9, "fill_squares broken\n");   // .length
          - flows back with the slice                                    
      142 -```                                                           
      143 -                                                              
      144 -Two more options, both heap-free: (a) **a span** ‚ÄĒ return offs
          -et+length `int`s into the caller's own                        
      145 -input, copying nothing (what `http.parse_request_line` writes 
          -onto each request row: `method_start` /                       
      146 -`method_len`); (b) **a pool** ‚ÄĒ when the result is "many rows,
          -" don't return it at all: a producer                          
      147 -system `insert`s the rows and a consumer reads them (see "Prod
          -ucer / consumer" above). The `[N]T`                           
      148 -pool is the bounded, static storage; the rows ARE the result. 
      149 -                                                              
      150 -A func may return a slice only when it traces to a buffer pass
          -ed _in_ ‚ÄĒ a slice of a fresh local is                         
      151 -rejected (see language.md, "Arrays and slices").              
      152 -                                                              
      153 -**Why:** "bounded" is the load-bearing word ‚ÄĒ a compile-time-k
          -nown size is what lets the slot be a                          
      154 -fixed stack or static reservation instead of a heap allocation
          -. The owned slice keeps the storage in                        
      155 -the caller's buffer; a span returns plain `int`s into the inpu
          -t; a pool holds the rows. None of these                       
      156 -is a `proc` ‚ÄĒ in arche, results that grow live in pools (rows)
          -, and effects live in systems over those                      
      157 -pools, not in a callee's out-params.

‚óŹ Here's where the main-removal landed, and the one decision that's blocking it.

  Progress

  - Codemod done (573 files): main :: proc() {‚Ä¶} ‚Üí entry :: system {‚Ä¶} + #run 
  entry (the name main collides with the emitted C @main, hence entry). A system
  can still call a helper proc during the transition, so files with helpers
  convert cleanly.
  - Found the real blocker: system/each/map bodies were never type-checked ‚ÄĒ
  tycheck.c only walked func/proc bodies. So x: int = "str", bad call args,
  break outside a loop, distinct-type mismatches all silently compiled inside a
  system. With everything moving into systems, that's a soundness hole. Fixed it
  (tycheck now walks system/each/map bodies) ‚ÄĒ recovered 17 of 27 regressions.
  Now at 856/866.

  The decision I need (it's a contradiction in the current type model)

  Type-checking system/map bodies surfaced that two committed tests disagree on
  whether a bare component (name :: int) is a distinct subtype or transparent:

  - sys_explicit_index_gather (a map): out = Table.val[v] ‚ÄĒ a val/int column
  read into an out column. Expects success. ‚Üí components transparent.
  - component_sibling_rejected: charge = m where m : mass. Expects rejection
  ("expected 'charge'"). ‚Üí components distinct.

  Same shape (component ‚Üí different component slot), opposite expectations.
  tycheck can't honor both ‚ÄĒ loosening for gather un-rejects component_sibling.
  Which is the rule?
  - Distinct (component_sibling is right): gather must write out(Table.val[v]),
  and I fix gather-style sites with explicit conversions.
  - Transparent for same-backing (gather is right): a raw backing value flows
  into an int-backed component freely; component_sibling's rejection then needs
  a stronger basis (it'd only reject across different backings or
  genuinely-distinct newtypes).

  The other 9 failures are downstream of this + the decl-kind change:
  opaque_into_column/double_drop (the once-now-checked opaque create-once seal)
  and 7 analyzer---dump/reachability tests (explicit_view/*, lint_unused_query)
  that key off main being the entry/root.

  Everything's captured in WIP_DECISIONS.md. Which way on component identity ‚ÄĒ
  distinct or transparent?

‚óŹ Agent "Language-consistency lens" finished ¬∑ 1m 40s

‚óŹ First agent (consistency) lands decisively on #self ‚ÄĒ a #-cursor intrinsic in
  the same family as #each_field. Two sharp findings: query headers are

‚úĽ Crunched for 8s

‚óŹ Agent "ECS/data-oriented semantics lens" finished ¬∑ 1m 32s

‚óŹ Second agent (ECS) found the real issue, deeper than syntax: the each fan
  iterates 0..count, but delete never decrements count (it only bumps generation
  + frees the slot) ‚ÄĒ so the fan over-iterates dead slots, and a second pass
  reprocesses them. That's the actual cause of "consumers re-process every
  pass," and it's a pre-existing bug (.count is documented as "live rows" but
  isn't). The fix is independent of syntax: the fan needs a per-slot liveness 
  predicate (skip tombstoned slots), and delete-current becomes a tombstone ‚ÄĒ
  not swap-remove (which would break handle stability + the generation model).

  Notably the two agents disagree on syntax: consistency says #self
  (value-shaped, delete(#self)(ok:)); ECS says consume; (no handle to stash ‚Üí
  avoids stale-handle reuse). That tension ‚ÄĒ is an exposed handle a footgun or a
  feature ‚ÄĒ is the crux. Waiting on ergonomics + implementation.

‚úĽ Brewed for 24s

‚óŹ Agent "Ergonomics/readability lens" finished ¬∑ 1m 38s

‚óŹ Third agent (ergonomics) surfaced a decisive new fact: arche already has a
  query-variable binding idiom ‚ÄĒ as $w (in the queries/joins design doc for
  nesting, currently TODO/unimplemented). That reframes "header binding": it
  isn't each (h: query{}) (which would collide with param: Type + E0037, as the
  consistency agent objected) ‚ÄĒ it's each (query {‚Ä¶} as $h), the existing 
  planned $w idiom. So candidate 2 is "finish the binding you already designed,"
  reuses both as $h and delete($h)(ok:), keeps the ok honest (vs consume; which
  hides it), and disambiguates nested drains by name.

  Standings so far (all noting the syntax is secondary to the liveness-predicate
  fix the ECS agent found):
  - Consistency: #self (didn't know about as $w)
  - ECS: consume; (no stashable handle ‚ÄĒ but a stale handle is
  generation-checked, a caught abort not UB, weakening this)
  - Ergonomics: as $h (reuse existing idiom, ok stays honest)

  Convergence forming around exposing a generation-checked handle (not the
  ok-hiding consume), via as $h or #self. Waiting on the implementation-cost
  agent to break the tie.

‚úĽ Brewed for 32s

‚ĚĮ but what if we support joins? two queries that have the same length? or will  
  that never be needed                                                          

‚óŹ That's the decisive question ‚ÄĒ and it breaks the tie. Joins already make a 
  single #self/consume insufficient, regardless of whether same-length joins
  ever ship:

  - Nested joins already exist and you already use them. router.resolve is each 
  (query {pattern, hid}) { each (query {path, ‚Ä¶}) { ‚Ä¶ } } ‚ÄĒ a cross-product
  join. There are two current rows at once (the outer Route row and the inner
  Req row). A single #self/consume can only mean one of them (the innermost), so
  it can't express "delete the outer match" vs "delete the inner match." It's
  ambiguous the moment you nest.
  - A same-length / multi-query join is the same problem, flattened. If a header
  ever binds two queries that advance together, each match is a tuple of rows.
  "Consume" has to say which pool's row(s) ‚ÄĒ one intrinsic can't.

  So the only surface that generalizes is per-query binding ‚ÄĒ each (query {‚Ä¶} as
  $a) { each (query {‚Ä¶} as $b) { delete($a)(ok:); /* or $b */ } }. Each
  query/level names its own row; you delete exactly the one(s) you mean. #self
  and consume; both hard-assume "exactly one current row," which is false under
  joins.

  This converges the three agents that have reported: the ergonomics agent
  already recommended as $h (and flagged it disambiguates nesting by name); the
  consistency agent's #self ranking was made without knowing as $w exists (it
  objected to a different form, h: query{}, which does collide with param: Type
  + E0037 ‚ÄĒ but as $h is the separate, already-planned $-binding); and the ECS
  agent's consume win was its no-stashable-handle property, weakened because a
  stale handle is generation-checked (a caught abort, not UB).

  So: joins make as $h (the existing $w query-variable binding) the right 
  surface ‚ÄĒ it's the only one that scales past one current row. Same-length
  joins don't even need to ship for this to hold; your nested each already needs
  it.

  Two things still independent of this:
  1. The real fix (ECS agent): the fan iterates 0..count but delete never
  decrements count, so it walks dead slots ‚ÄĒ the fan needs a per-slot liveness
  check to skip tombstoned rows. That's required for any drain to work, whatever
  the syntax.
  2. The implementation-cost agent is still running; I'll fold its touch-point
  estimate in, but it won't change the join argument.

  Want me to go with as $h + the liveness fix?

  Your join question is the tiebreaker the whole thing turned on: #self/consume;

‚ĚĮ could the same syntax be used for future resource stuff? like the once

‚óŹ Good instinct ‚ÄĒ that's the relational/ownership form (as $w was proposed
  precisely for nesting/relationships). Let me check what was actually designed
  so I can say whether it's the same mechanism:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Yes ‚ÄĒ definitively. That proposal is in the design doc right now, and it's the
  same as $w (docs/design/queries-systems-and-joins.md:202-220):

  each (query { handle } as $w) {                          // bind the matched
  window to $w
    each (query { pos, color, r } where window == $w) {    // only THIS window's
  drawables
      ‚Ä¶
    }
  }

  So as $w is the planned query variable (it calls it "flecs-style $w") for
  relationships ‚ÄĒ "this drawable belongs to that window." The binding does one
  thing: it names the matched row/entity as $w. What you then do with $w is
  open:
  - consume it ‚ÄĒ delete($w)(ok:) (our case), or
  - use it as a relationship key ‚ÄĒ where component == $w (the resource/ownership
  case you're remembering).

  That's the unification you're after: as $h for the current-row handle IS the 
  $w query variable. Same mechanism, just a different use of the bound value.
  Which means:

  - #self / consume; are dead ends for this. They name "the one current row,
  delete-only" ‚ÄĒ you can't write where window == #self, and consume; has no
  value to key a relationship on. They don't generalize to relationships; as $h
  is literally the relationship surface.
  - Building as $h now lays the foundation, not a throwaway. The doc lists what
  relationships need: "a relationship component type (an entity reference),
  query variables ($w), and the join solver." Our consume feature builds the
  query-variable ($w) binding half ‚ÄĒ exactly the piece both features share.
  Relationships then add the where ‚Ä¶ == $w filter + the entity-reference
  component on top.

  So the analysis + your recollection point the same way, harder: as $h (= $w)
  is the one binding idiom for naming a matched row/entity, serving consume,
  joins-via-nesting, and the future relationship/resource model. The work splits
  cleanly:

  1. liveness fix in codegen_each_fan (skip tombstoned slots) ‚ÄĒ needed for any
  drain, fixes a latent bug.
  2. $w query-variable binding (each (query {‚Ä¶} as $w) ‚Üí a handle(driver) local)
  ‚ÄĒ consume uses it via delete($w)(ok:) today; relationships extend it with
  where ‚Ä¶ == $w later.

  Shall I start ‚ÄĒ liveness fix first, then the $w binding?

      10102 +   * handle could alias a fresh entity. Abort like a stack o
            +verflow would. (Bit 31 is the dead/liveness                 
      10103 +   * flag, so the generation is 31 bits; a live slot's store
            +d_gen is positive, maxing at 0x7FFFFFFF.) */                
      10104 +  buffer_append(ctx, "  %gen_maxed = icmp eq i32 %stored_gen
            +, 2147483647\n");                                           
      10105    buffer_append(ctx, "  br i1 %gen_maxed, label %gen_exhaust
             ed, label %do_free\n\n");
      10106    /* Generation exhausted: a resource limit, reported as a v
             alue (ok=0) rather than aborting. */
      10107    buffer_append(ctx, "gen_exhausted:\n");

‚óŹ Liveness fix done ‚ÄĒ regression-tested, all green (24/24 in the delete-heavy
  dirs + fmt). Now the binding syntax. Let me explore sigil options grounded in
  arche's actual grammar ‚ÄĒ check what $/?/etc. currently lex as, and how the
  header parses:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Grounded in the lexer/grammar:
  - $ is currently unused ‚ÄĒ available, no conflicts.
  - ? is taken (policy sigil ‚ÄĒ ?reject), so it's out.
  - @/# are taken (decorators / intrinsics+directives).
  - & only exists as &&; a lone & reads as address-of, which arche deliberately
  doesn't have ‚ÄĒ off-brand.
  - The query header is bare component names (SysParams ‚Üź "(" Identifier ("," 
  ‚Ä¶)* ")"); there's no as clause yet ‚ÄĒ it's the doc's TODO. So whatever we pick,
  we're adding the as ‚Ä¶ clause fresh.

  The real question isn't which sigil ‚ÄĒ it's whether the bound thing needs a 
  sigil at all. It's a handle(Driver) ‚ÄĒ a first-class value. Three coherent
  options:

  1. as $w (the doc's current plan ‚ÄĒ sigil).
  each (query { handle } as $w) { ‚Ä¶ delete($w)(ok:); }
  each (query { pos } where window == $w) { ‚Ä¶ }      // relationship use
  The $ marks "query/entity variable" at every use site ‚ÄĒ unmistakable in a
  where col == $w filter that it's the bound row, not a local. Cost: sigil
  noise, and it reads as inherited-from-flecs.

  2. as w (no sigil ‚ÄĒ arche-native binding).
  each (query { handle } as w) { ‚Ä¶ delete(w)(ok:); }
  each (query { pos } where window == w) { ‚Ä¶ }
  w is just a handle(Driver) local; the as keyword does the marking at the 
  binding site. Relationship filters become ordinary handle comparisons (window 
  == w), and the join is the solver's optimization, not a syntactic distinction
  ‚ÄĒ which is exactly the freedom you noted arche has that flecs doesn't (flecs
  needs $ because it has no real binding grammar; we do). Cost: at a use site w
  looks like any local (the as is where you learn it's the row); a column named
  w would shadow.

  3. &w ‚ÄĒ off-brand (arche has no reference/address syntax), skip.

  My lean: option 2 (as w, no sigil). The bound value is a real handle, not a
  DSL placeholder; as already signals the binding; and the relationship form
  falls out as a plain handle comparison the solver indexes ‚ÄĒ you're not
  constrained to flecs's sigil because you have the grammar flecs lacks. $ only
  earns its keep if you want query-variables to be a visibly distinct kind at
  every use (which has some merit in deeply-nested where filters).

  Which way ‚ÄĒ as w or as $w?

‚úĽ Worked for 10m 3s

‚ĚĮ what does  each imply? what does the fundemental grammar imply?               

‚óŹ Let me reason it from the grammar's essence rather than the menu.

  What each implies. each (query { pos, vel }) { ‚Ä¶ } binds the columns by bare
  name ‚ÄĒ pos is this row's pos. That's the data-oriented choice: you work with
  column data, and the entity/row identity is deliberately not surfaced. So when
  you ask for the row's handle, you're stepping out of the common (data) path
  into the structural path ‚ÄĒ identity, for delete/relationships. each implies
  one current element per iteration; its data is bare-named; its identity is,
  today, unnamed.

  What the fundamental grammar implies ‚ÄĒ this is the decisive part. Look at what
  carries a sigil in arche and what doesn't:
  - # ‚Üí compiler intrinsics / directives (#run, #each_field, #import)
  - @ ‚Üí decorators (@drop, @gpu)
  - bare name ‚Üí everything that is a value or a binding: locals, columns,
  declarations, and handles (insert(P{‚Ä¶})(h:, ok:) ‚ÄĒ h is a bare name; delete(h)
  ‚ÄĒ bare).

  A row handle is a handle(Driver) value. The grammar reserves sigils for
  compiler kinds, never for ordinary values ‚ÄĒ and handles are already bare names
  everywhere they appear (h from insert, the arg to delete). So $w would be the
  only sigil arche puts on a plain value ‚ÄĒ it imports flecs's convention into a
  grammar that has no slot for it. $ is the answer to a constraint arche
  doesn't have.

  So the grammar implies: a bare name, no sigil. The binding is just another
  name binding, the same kind as a column or an insert handle.

  That leaves only where the name goes, and each's own meaning answers it: the
  handle is the iterand's identity, and arche already binds the iterand's data
  inside the query. So bind its identity there too ‚ÄĒ

  each (query { pos, vel } as w) {     // w : handle(driver) ‚ÄĒ the matched row
  itself
    pos.x += vel.x;                     // columns: the row's data (bare, as
  today)
    delete(w)(ok:);                     // w: the row's identity (bare handle,
  like insert's h)
  }
  each (query { drawable } where window == w) { ‚Ä¶ }   // relationship: a plain
  handle compare

  as w reads as "the query's matched row, bound as w" ‚ÄĒ the row is what the 
  query selects, so binding it on the query (not a leading each w (‚Ä¶)) keeps
  "the query is the binding spec" intact. as marks the structural intent (you're
  naming an identity, not data); w is then an ordinary handle that flows into
  delete(w)(ok:) exactly like insert's handle ‚ÄĒ keeping the ok honest, no new
  value-kind.

  Conclusion from the grammar, not the menu: as w, no sigil. $ only makes sense
  if arche lacked a binding grammar ‚ÄĒ it doesn't; the row handle is a
  first-class value and arche names values bare.

  Shall I build each (query {‚Ä¶} as w) ‚Üí a handle(driver) local, consumed by
  delete(w)(ok:)?

  Read 1 file, ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Done ‚ÄĒ the whole feature is built and green.

  each (query {‚Ä¶} as w) ‚ÄĒ current-row handle + consume

  Settled the syntax from the grammar, not a menu: sigils in arche mark compiler
  kinds (# intrinsics, @ decorators), never values ‚ÄĒ and a row handle is a
  first-class handle(driver) value, named bare everywhere (like insert's h). So
  no $ ‚ÄĒ as w binds a plain handle. The relationship form (where component == w)
  falls out as an ordinary handle compare the solver indexes; $ only exists in
  Flecs because it lacks a binding grammar.

  Two parts, both landed (869 lit + doctests + corpus + fmt all green):

  1. Liveness fix (the real bug you spotted ‚ÄĒ "re-runs the same rows"). The each
  fan iterated 0..count, but delete frees a slot without shrinking count, so
  dead holes got re-walked (stale re-processing; a drain never emptied). Fixed
  with a sign-bit liveness flag in the existing gen_counters ‚ÄĒ delete sets bit
  31 (gen < 0 = dead), insert clears it, the fan skips negative-gen slots. No
  new struct field; handle validation is unchanged (a dead slot never matches a
  live handle ‚Üí still aborts). Regression: each_skips_deleted_rows.
  2. as w binding across all 6 layers: SN_QUERY_BIND node, contextual as in the
  each header, HirEachDecl.row_var, semantic binds w as tyid_of_handle(driver),
  codegen mints the handle (slot | gen<<32) in the fan. delete(w)(ok:) consumes
  the current row. Regression: each_as_bind_consume.

  And it fixes the thing you flagged twice ‚ÄĒ docs/patterns.md's
  producer/consumer now actually consumes: each consumer deletes its row, so a
  second pass drains nothing.

  drain :: each (query { n } as w) {
    fmt.printf("do %d\n", n);
    delete(w)(_:);          // consume ‚ÄĒ the row leaves the pool
  }
  // run twice: first pass do 1/2/3, second pass nothing.

  This is also the foundation for the relationship/$w work (where component == w
  + entity-reference component + join solver) ‚ÄĒ the binding half is now built.

‚úĽ Crunched for 1m 15s

‚ĚĮ wwhat is a span                                                               

‚óŹ A span is just two integers ‚ÄĒ an offset and a length ‚ÄĒ that point into an
  existing buffer, instead of copying the bytes out into their own storage.

  Concrete: a request row has raw :: [8192]char (the bytes net.recv filled). The
  request line is "GET /a HTTP/1.1". The method "GET" lives at raw[0..3].
  Rather than copy "GET" into its own method :: [8]char buffer, you record:

  method_start = 0    // scalar int columns on the row
  method_len   = 3

  That pair is the span. When you actually need the method, you slice the parent
  buffer: raw[method_start : method_start + method_len] ‚Üí "GET". Nothing was
  copied ‚ÄĒ the bytes stay in raw; the span just says where they are.

  That's exactly what http.parse_request_line already writes
  (method_start/method_len, target_start/target_len) ‚ÄĒ and it's arche's existing
  idiom for "return a substring without a heap" (it's how str.find_char +
  slicing work).

  The relevance to the open decision: to route the target to the router and the
  file-path builder, option A (spans) passes those two ints and each consumer
  slices raw[start:len] itself ‚ÄĒ zero copy. Option B (buffer) copies the target
  bytes into a separate path :: [N]char column (via an Eff that fills it) so the
  consumers get a ready-made []char. Spans are cheaper and fit the "buffers are
  only filled by Eff out-slots, never hand-copied" rule; the cost is
  router.resolve takes (raw, start, len) instead of a path column.

  Want the span route?

‚úĽ Brewed for 20s

‚ĚĮ why char? why not byte?                                                       

‚óŹ Both are 8-bit (effectively u8, 0‚Äď255) ‚ÄĒ the difference is semantic intent,
  not size:

  - char is the text element: []char is arche's string type, and it's what plugs
  into str.* (strlen, find_char), %s, and string literals ("GET").
  - byte is a raw octet ‚ÄĒ binary data, no text meaning.

  So you're right to flag it: a net.recv buffer or an mmap'd file is raw bytes,
  and []byte is the honest type. I used char because (a) HTTP requests and argv
  are text, and (b) []char is the only type the string machinery (str.strlen,
  %s, span-comparison against "GET") works on. That convenience conflates
  "octet" with "character."

  The clean model is the separation you're pointing at:
  - raw I/O ‚Üí []byte ‚ÄĒ net.recv, io.read, io.file_map, mem.bound return bytes (a
  file or socket is bytes, possibly binary).
  - text ‚Üí []char ‚ÄĒ only once you decide the bytes are text do you view them as
  characters.
  - a free reinterpret between them (same storage) at that boundary, since both
  are u8.

  The cost is real: today the text tooling (str.find_char, %s, "GET" literals)
  is char-typed, so HTTP parsing on a []byte recv buffer would need a byte‚Üíchar
  view at the point you treat the line as text. That's arguably correct ‚ÄĒ it
  marks "I'm now interpreting these bytes as text," exactly like mem.bound marks
  "I'm now treating this address as memory."

  So the decision under the buffer question is really: do raw I/O buffers become
  []byte (with a byte‚ÜĒchar reinterpret where text starts), or stay []char for 
  convenience? My lean is []byte for the raw side ‚ÄĒ it's the same honesty
  argument as rawptr. Want raw I/O retyped to byte?

‚úĽ Brewed for 47s

‚ĚĮ yes. why is span needed? what happened to the bound fmap?                     

‚óŹ Confirmed on byte. And your span question is the sharp one ‚ÄĒ spans are a
  workaround, and they're the same shape as bound's output.

  A slice is a view = (pointer, length). mem.bound(p, n) produces one from a
  foreign pointer+length; raw[a:b] produces one from a buffer+range. A span
  (method_start, method_len as two int columns) is just that same view stored as
  two integers instead of a fat pointer ‚ÄĒ and then re-materialized as
  raw[start:len] at every use. I reached for it assuming you can't carry a slice
  from the parse system to the router system (data between systems flows
  through columns, and I assumed a column can't hold a slice).

  If a pool column can hold a slice ‚ÄĒ a {ptr, len} into the row's own raw, which
  is stable because pools are static and never move ‚ÄĒ then spans are
  unnecessary: the parse stores method :: []byte (a real slice into raw), and
  the router/build_path read it directly. Same "view" concept as bound, no
  offset-juggling, no re-slicing. Let me check whether that actually works
  today:

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ There's your answer, in the error: a slice column is read-only in a query ‚ÄĒ
  view = raw[0:5] is rejected, the same restriction as buffer columns. Only
  scalar columns (ints) are mutable in a query. So to carry a substring from the
  parse system to the router system, I'm forced to store it as two mutable ints
  (a span) and re-slice. Spans are a workaround for "you can't write a slice 
  into a column."

  bound didn't go anywhere ‚ÄĒ it's still the FFI view (rawptr ‚Üí slice). The gap
  is one level down: a view (ptr+len) ‚ÄĒ whether it comes from bound or from
  raw[a:b] ‚ÄĒ currently can't be stored in a column and passed between systems.
  That's the only reason spans exist.

  The real fix is the one you're circling: make slice/view columns writable.
  view :: []byte storing a {ptr, len} into the row's own raw is sound ‚ÄĒ pools
  are static, so the pointer never moves; the view lives exactly as long as the
  pool. It needs the same guard arche already has for returns ("the slice must
  trace to stable storage, not a fresh local" ‚ÄĒ raw[a:b] traces to a pool buffer
  ‚úď; local[a:b] would dangle ‚úó). With that, the parse writes method = raw[a:b]
  directly, the router reads method, and spans disappear ‚ÄĒ a view is a view,
  whether bound-minted from a pointer or sliced from a buffer.

  So the unification is: byte for raw storage, views (slices) as the one 
  substring type ‚ÄĒ flowing through columns like any data ‚ÄĒ and bound is just the
  FFI-boundary case of minting a view. No spans, no offset-juggling.

  This is a real feature (writable view-columns) rather than a decision to make.
  Want me to build it ‚ÄĒ writable slice columns (a view into stable pool
  storage) ‚ÄĒ which then lets the web server (and parse_request_line) drop spans
  entirely?

‚óŹ Tested both ways and the current compiler rejects each ‚ÄĒ so let me make sure

  - Move a fresh buffer in ‚Üí rejected: arche has no heap, so an owned [4]int is
  a stack local that dies at the system's return, and moving it into a

‚úĽ Worked for 3m 58s

‚ĚĮ you aren't making much sense. Maybe you are thinking about current            
  capabilities too much. Because im thinking about the design itself. Tell me   
  what the semantics should do and why for each method                          

‚óŹ You're right ‚ÄĒ let me drop "what compiles today" and reason about what the
  semantics should be. There's one underlying object ‚ÄĒ a view (a window into a
  buffer) ‚ÄĒ and three ways to model passing one between systems. Here's what
  each should mean and why.

  Span (two ints: offset + length).
  Semantics: store the offset and length; the substring is whatever
  raw[off:off+len] evaluates to at the point of use. What it should do: nothing
  on its own ‚ÄĒ it's a view with the buffer erased. The two ints don't know which
  buffer they index, don't know if it's still valid, and force every consumer
  to re-state "slice this buffer." That's the failure: it throws away the one
  fact the type system should keep ‚ÄĒ this is a window into that specific buffer
  ‚ÄĒ and replaces a checked borrow with an unchecked pair of numbers. A span is
  the workaround you reach for when the language won't let a view flow as data.
  The design answer: don't model substrings as spans.

  View / borrow (the substring is a slice that borrows its source).
  Semantics: a view carries both the buffer it points into and the range, and it
  is a borrow ‚ÄĒ valid exactly as long as its source buffer is. What it should
  do: be a first-class value that flows through a column like any other, with
  the borrow-lifetime tracked. In a request row, method = raw[a:b] makes method
  a view borrowing the row's raw; the two are co-located, so method lives and
  dies with the row ‚ÄĒ the borrow can never dangle. That's the right model: a
  substring is a window into existing bytes, and a borrow says precisely that,
  zero-copy, checked. This is also exactly what bound produces ‚ÄĒ a view minted
  from a foreign pointer ‚ÄĒ so bound and raw[a:b] become the same kind of thing,
  just minted from different sources.

  Owned copy (the column owns its own bytes; you move/copy the substring in).
  Semantics: duplicate the substring into the column's own storage; it now owns
  those bytes and outlives raw. What it should do: be available, but only when a
  substring must escape its source ‚ÄĒ survive after the buffer is gone or
  reused. The parse case never needs that (the row keeps raw the whole time), so
  paying a copy here is wrong by default; it's the tool for "I need this to
  outlive its origin," not "I want to name a window into it."

  The unifying rule ‚ÄĒ and where your "move ownership" fits. Two distinct roles:
  - Ownership is for storage ‚ÄĒ the actual bytes. recv yields owned bytes; that
  ownership moves into the row's raw column; the row now owns it and is
  responsible for it (its @drop fires on row delete). Ownership answers who 
  holds the storage and when is it freed.
  - Borrowing is for views ‚ÄĒ substrings. A view borrows owned storage; its
  lifetime may not exceed the owner's. Borrowing answers who's looking at the 
  storage and for how long.

  So the semantics: buffers are owned (ownership moves through the pipeline); 
  substrings are borrows (views) into them, lifetime ‚Č§ the buffer's. A column
  can hold a borrow exactly when the borrowed storage lives at least as long as
  the column ‚ÄĒ trivially true for a view into the same row's raw (co-lifetime),
  true for any pool buffer (pool-lifetime), false for a stack local (can't
  outlive its frame). That last clause is the only safety rule, and it's the
  same borrow rule arche already applies to returns.

  Net: spans are an erased borrow and shouldn't be the model; the substring type
  is a view that borrows its source, with lifetime tied to the owned buffer;
  owned-copy is the escape hatch for when a view must outlive its origin. Is
  that the model you're after?

‚úĽ Brewed for 1m 8s

‚ĚĮ span will probably lead to use after free, no?                                
  ‚éŅ ¬†Read docs/patterns.md (111 lines)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Read tests/unit/language/each/each_as_bind_consume.arche (33 lines)  ‚éŅ ¬†Read syntax/syntax_tree.c (288 lines)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Referenced file codegen/codegen.c
  ‚éŅ ¬†Plan file referenced (~/.claude/plans/tender-meandering-pike.md)

‚óŹ Yes ‚ÄĒ and that's the decisive argument, sharper than the ergonomic one I gave.

  A span is two ints. Integers have no lifetime and no borrow relationship ‚ÄĒ the
  type system sees offset and length, not "a borrow of raw." So nothing in the
  language ties the span's validity to raw staying alive. Delete the row, reuse
  the buffer, let raw's frame exit ‚ÄĒ the span is still two perfectly
  valid-looking numbers, and the next raw[off:off+len] reads freed or recycled
  bytes. Use-after-free, and the compiler can't see it coming, because there's
  no edge between the span and the storage to check.

  That's exactly the property a view/borrow has and a span throws away: a borrow
  is the lifetime edge ‚ÄĒ "this view is valid only while its source is." With
  that edge, "buffer outlives every view into it" is a checkable rule, and UAF
  is structurally impossible (in the same-row case, view and buffer are
  co-lifetime, so it's automatic). A span is an unchecked raw pointer wearing an
  int costume; it reintroduces precisely the manual-lifetime hazard arche's
  ownership model exists to kill.

  So this isn't a tie-breaker, it's disqualifying: spans turn a 
  compiler-enforced safety property into a manual discipline. The substring has
  to be a borrow-carrying view, because only the view keeps the lifetime edge
  that prevents the UAF you're pointing at. Spans are acceptable in exactly one
  place ‚ÄĒ fully inside a single system where the buffer provably can't change
  between slice and use ‚ÄĒ and should never be the thing that flows between
  systems through a column.

‚úĽ Cooked for 2m 42s

‚ĚĮ right so do the views into the raw data need to be writable or not?           

‚óŹ No ‚ÄĒ and "read-only" isn't a concession here, it's the property that makes the
  whole pattern sound.

  Walk the data flow: raw is written once, by whoever produces it (recv fills
  the buffer / the row takes ownership of the bytes). From that moment it's
  frozen. method, target, version are windows you cut into those frozen bytes so
  downstream systems can read them to route. Nobody writes "GET" back through
  the method view to edit the request. The producer writes the storage; the
  views only read it.

  And it has to be that way, because of shared-xor-mutable:

  - A read-only view is a shared borrow. You can have many at once ‚ÄĒ method,
  target, and version all borrowing the same raw simultaneously is fine, because
  none of them can mutate it, so they can't conflict. That coexistence is the 
  entire point ‚ÄĒ one buffer, many named windows.
  - A writable view would be an exclusive borrow. You can't have two exclusive
  borrows of raw alive at once, so the moment you make views writable you can
  have at most one view into raw at a time ‚ÄĒ which destroys the multi-substring
  pattern, and reintroduces aliasing-while-mutating, the exact hazard the
  ownership model exists to prevent.

  So writability and multiplicity are mutually exclusive, and the parse case
  wants multiplicity. Views into raw are read-only shared borrows.

  Where does writing happen, then? Two places, never through a view:
  1. Producing raw ‚ÄĒ its owning system fills it once (the out-slot / move-in),
  then it's read-only for the rest of the row's life. This is the same "a buffer
  is filled exactly once, by its producer" idiom as sprintf-into-a-buffer.
  2. Transforming bytes (percent-decode a target, lowercase a header) ‚ÄĒ you
  write into a new owned buffer (a separate column), whose producer fills it
  once; that buffer then has its own read-only views. You never mutate raw in
  place through an alias.

  So the rule is clean: owned buffers are write-once by their producer; views 
  are read-only borrows into them. Mutation always means "produce a fresh owned
  buffer," never "write through a view." That keeps every view co-lifetime and
  non-aliasing with its source, which is what kills the UAF and the data-race at
  once.

‚úĽ Cogitated for 31s

‚ĚĮ so what is the problem, this is already how slices work, no?                  

‚óŹ Right ‚ÄĒ that's the realization. There's no new concept here. A slice is a
  read-only borrow of its source: (ptr, len), you can't write through it, it's
  valid while its source is. That's precisely the substring we want. So the
  substring is just a []char, stored in a []char column. Spans were solving a 
  problem that slices already solve ‚ÄĒ I talked myself into a workaround for a
  non-issue.

  So the design answer is: method :: []char, target :: []char, each holding
  raw[a:b]. Done. No span, no new "view" type ‚ÄĒ it was always slices.

  The only thing that's genuinely not free ‚ÄĒ and it's a lifetime matter, not a
  representation one ‚ÄĒ is that the borrow-check arche already does for a slice
  inside one function scope has to also hold when the slice lives in a column
  and is read by a different system:

  - Same-row view (method borrows the same row's raw): co-lifetime ‚ÄĒ method and
  raw are created and deleted together, so the borrow can never outlive its
  source. This is automatically safe and needs nothing new. This is the parse
  case.
  - Cross-row view (a slice column in row A pointing into row B's buffer, B
  independently deletable): the one case that needs the rule ‚ÄĒ and it's the same
  rule arche already enforces for returns, "a view can't outlive its source,"
  just applied across the column boundary.

  And the mechanical bit: writing a slice into a column is an output, so in the
  each the target column is an out-slot while raw is a read-input ‚ÄĒ same shape
  as filling any buffer column, not a new mechanism.

  So: nothing fundamental is missing. The substring is a slice; the http code's
  *_start/*_len int pairs collapse into []char columns; the only thing to
  actually enforce is the cross-row lifetime edge (free in the same-row case the
  parser actually uses). Want me to rewrite parse_request_line to write []char
  slice columns instead of spans and confirm it round-trips?

‚úĽ Cogitated for 56s

‚ĚĮ lets do some serious analysis  and research.Surely another language or        
  something has run into this problem.                                          

‚óŹ This is the right instinct ‚ÄĒ this is a classic, well-trodden PL problem, and
  the span-vs-slice framing I gave is probably a false dichotomy. Let me
  research the prior art seriously rather than reason in a vacuum. I'll fan out
  parallel research on the languages/systems that hit exactly this ‚ÄĒ "store a
  borrowed view into a buffer, with safety, no GC" ‚ÄĒ and then synthesize against
  arche's specifics (stable pools, generational handles, no heap).

‚óŹ 4 background agents launched (‚Üď to manage)
   ‚Ēú Research Cyclone and regions
   ‚Ēú Research Rust slices/arenas/rustc spans
   ‚Ēú Research ECS handles and relative pointers
   ‚ĒĒ Research Swift Span and C++ view dangling

‚óŹ I've launched four parallel research threads on the systems that hit this
  exact problem ‚ÄĒ "store/pass a borrowed view into a buffer, safely, without
  GC":

  1. Cyclone + region-based memory management (Tofte‚ÄďTalpin, ML Kit) ‚ÄĒ regions
  as program-lifetime arenas, fat pointers, region polymorphism as type-level
  lifetimes; how it maps to arche's pools.
  2. Rust + rustc ‚ÄĒ the self-referential-struct prohibition, the "use indices
  not references" idiom, generational arenas, and crucially why rustc itself 
  stores integer Spans (BytePos) into an immortal SourceMap instead of &str.
  3. Data-oriented / ECS ‚ÄĒ "handles are the better pointers," generational
  indices (slotmap/EnTT/flecs/Bevy), relative/based pointers (Odin,
  FlatBuffers/Cap'n Proto offset pointers).
  4. Swift Span/~Escapable + C++ string_view/span ‚ÄĒ Swift's brand-new
  non-escapable views with lifetime dependencies (memory-safe borrows, no full
  lifetimes, no GC ‚ÄĒ almost exactly our target), versus the C++ dangling-view
  footgun, plus Vale's generational references.

  The thesis I expect them to sharpen: span-vs-slice was a false dichotomy. The
  data-oriented world's answer is almost certainly a third point ‚ÄĒ a
  handle-qualified view = (owning-row generational-handle, offset, len) ‚ÄĒ which
  carries the provenance and the runtime UAF check that bare spans lack, is
  relocatable/serializable unlike a raw fat pointer, and degenerates to a free 
  bare offset in the same-row co-lifetime case the parser actually uses. rustc's
  integer-span choice and ECS handles both point here; Swift's Span tells us
  the compile-time story for the passed-not-stored case.

  They're running in the background; I'll synthesize the findings into the
  design once they report back, then write up the plan.

‚úĽ Saut√©ed for 2m 36s

‚óŹ Agent "Research Swift Span and C++ view dangling" finished ¬∑ 3m 15s

‚óŹ First thread back (Swift/C++/Go/Vale) ‚ÄĒ and it lands a sharp thesis I didn't
  expect going in:

  ‚Ėé Views are unstorable by design. Swift's ~Escapable Span forbids storing a 
  ‚Ėé view in any long-lived container ‚ÄĒ that prohibition is the safety property. 
  ‚Ėé So the rule becomes: store handles, pass views, generation-check at the 
  ‚Ėé boundary where a view would cross into persisted storage.

  That reframes our whole "should the column hold a slice" question. The answer
  the prior art suggests: no ‚ÄĒ a column should never hold a raw view. What
  persists in a column is a generational handle (which arche already has); the
  (ptr,len) view is a transient projection you re-derive from the handle within
  a scope, paying one generation check at the moment of rematerialization.
  Compile-time safety where the source is in-scope (Swift ~Escapable); a runtime
  gen-check (Vale-style ‚ÄĒ the same primitive as arche's handles) exactly where
  a static lifetime can't reach. And C++ string_view is the cautionary tale:
  this must be a hard type-system rule, never a lint ‚ÄĒ which matches your
  hard-errors-over-lints stance.

  Three threads still running (Cyclone/regions, Rust/rustc spans, ECS handles).
  I'll hold synthesis until they're in, then write the design ‚ÄĒ I expect rustc's
  integer-Span choice and the ECS "handles not pointers" idiom to corroborate
  the same conclusion from different directions.

  ‚ĒĆ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ē¨‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚ĒÄ‚Ēź
  ‚Ēā                ‚Ēā  (A) bare   ‚Ēā  (B) raw fat   ‚Ēā (C) handle-qualified  ‚Ēā
